The Definition and Condemnation of Mukābara
Author defines stubbornness and explains why it is the worst interruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of stubbornness (mukābara) in jadal and explain why it is considered the worst form of interruption.

Core Idea
Stubbornness is the worst interruption because it prioritises victory over truth.

In this lesson, we examine the first major form of interruption in dialectical debate: stubbornness. The author defines it sharply and condemns it as the ugliest and most foolish behaviour. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing legitimate debate from sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of mukābara
Find the sentence that defines stubbornness. Notice the contrast: 'not to manifest truth but to force the opponent.'
2
Identify the core motive
The author says the debater's focus is on overcoming the opponent by any means, not on truth. Even if truth becomes clear, he persists.
3
Understand the condemnation
Note the strong language: 'worst form', 'ugliest', 'most indicative of foolishness', 'no benefit from his speech.'
4
Extract the implication
Any debater who can't win by argument and resorts to stubbornness is considered 'interrupted' (munqaṭi'). This means he loses the debate not by rule but by abandonment of fair play.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونتناول فيما يلي جملة من صور الانقطاع الهامة بالشرح والتفصيل :
أ - الانقطاع بالمكابرة :
المكابرة في الجدل : هي المنازعة في المسألة العلمية، لا لإظهار الصواب بل لإلزام الخصم. أي أن المجادل لا يكون همه منصبا حول النتيجة العلمية أو الحق في المسألة موضع الجدل، وإنما يكون همه منصبًا حول كيفية الغلبة على خصمه بأي وسيلة سنحت، ومعنى هذا أنه حتى إذا ما اتضح له الحق على لسان خصمه، فإنه يبقى مستمرًا في الجدل بغرض الغلبة على خصمه. ولذلك يعتبر منقطعًا كل مجادل يعجز عن الغلبة باستعمال الحجة ويتخذ المكابرة سبيلا له، وهو شر وجوه الانقطاع، وأقبحها وأدلها على سخف صاحبه، وقلة مبالاته بما يظهر من فضيحته وتخليطه في ديانته، وليس يُنتفع بكلام كانت عادته أن يحمل نفسه على المكابرة.
والمكابرة تعرض للخصم في أمور : منها : أن يقول شيئًا ، فإذا رأى ما يلزم عليه جحد أن يكون قاله وصمم على ذلك وكابر فيه، وهذا الضرب من المكابرة يقع كثيرًا بين الخصوم. ومنها : أن يجحد مذهبًا له أو الرئيس الذي ينتحل قوله . ومنها : أن يجحد ضرورة يشترك أهل العقول فيها، ويدعي أن الحقيقة معه جحدها، وإنما يطلقون ما أطلقوا من إثباتها على جهة المجاز دون الحقيقة.
We will discuss in the following a number of important forms of interruption with elaboration and detail:
A - Interruption by stubbornness:
Stubbornness in dialectics: it is disputing in a scholarly issue, not to manifest truth but to force the opponent. That is, the debater's focus is not on the scientific result or the truth in the issue under discussion, but his focus is on how to overcome his opponent by any means available. The meaning of this is that even if the truth becomes clear to him through his opponent's speech, he continues the debate for the purpose of defeating his opponent. Therefore, every debater who is unable to overcome by using the argument and adopts stubbornness as his way is considered interrupted. It is the worst form of interruption, the ugliest, and the most indicative of the foolishness of its practitioner, his lack of concern for the disgrace and confusion in his religion that appears from him. No benefit is gained from the speech of one whose habit is to drive himself to stubbornness. Stubbornness occurs to the opponent in matters: among them: that he says something, then when he sees what follows from it, he denies having said it and insists on that and persists in stubbornness. This type of stubbornness often occurs among opponents. Among them: that he denies a school of his or the leader whose statement he adopts. Among them: that he denies a necessity that rational people share in, and claims that the truth is with him in denying it, and that they only assert what they assert of its affirmation metaphorically, not literally.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the first important form of interruption: stubbornness (mukābarah). The definition makes clear that the stubborn debater cares only about victory, not truth. The author then lists three specific sub-types: (1) denying one's own statement when its implications become clear, (2) denying one's own school or its founder, and (3) denying a rational necessity while claiming that affirmations of it are metaphorical. Stubbornness is condemned as the worst form due to its moral and intellectual corruption.

Key Terms
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a debater who uses stubbornness considered 'interrupted'?
A) He loses the argument by rules of logic.
B) He is unable to overcome his opponent by using argument and resorts to stubbornness.
C) He has a weak memory.
D) He refuses to speak anymore.

Key Takeaway
Stubbornness is the abandonment of truth-seeking in debate, and it is the worst form of interruption because it corrupts the purpose of jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine a debate transcript (given by instructor) and identify at least one statement that exemplifies mukābara based on the definition.

The Three Sub-Types of Mukābara
Author enumerates three specific forms of stubbornness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognise the three sub-types of stubbornness mentioned by the author and identify them in debate scenarios.

Core Idea
Mukābara manifests in three specific forms: denying one's own statement, denying one's school or leader, and denying rational necessities.

The author, after defining stubbornness, lists three concrete ways it appears in debate. Understanding these sub-types helps in diagnosing dishonest debate tactics. In this lesson, we unpack each one and see how they violate the norms of jadal.

Guided Path
1
List the three sub-types
Scan the passage after 'والمكابرة تعرض للخصم في أمور' and identify each: (1) denying one's own statement when implications are seen, (2) denying one's school or leader, (3) denying a rational necessity.
2
Analyse the first type
A debater says something, then when its logical implications are pointed out, he denies having said it. This is a classic use of stubbornness to avoid logical consequences.
3
Analyse the second type
A debater denies adherence to his own school or its founder to avoid an argument. This is a form of betrayal of intellectual commitment.
4
Analyse the third type
Denying a rational necessity that all rational people acknowledge, claiming the affirmation is metaphorical. This is a rejection of self-evident truths.
5
Compare the three
All share the motive of victory over truth, but the third is perhaps most extreme as it rejects basic reason.

Source Text

Interruption by Contradiction of School (al-Munaqada al-Madhhab)
Author explains contradiction of school as a milder form of interruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between interruption by contradiction of school (al-munaqada al-madhhab) and stubbornness (al-mukabara) in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Contradicting your own school is a milder interruption than stubbornness because you retract when you see the implication.

In the discipline of jadal (dialectical debate), a debater may be unable to defend his own stated position and switches to the opposite view. This is called 'interruption by contradiction of school' (al-munaqada al-madhhab). Unlike the stubborn debater who persists in falsehood, this debater retracts upon recognizing the logical force of the opponent's argument. Though still an interruption, it is considered less blameworthy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Find the debater's original position (mudda'i) in the exchange. In the passage, the debater holds a certain statement in the issue but cannot support it.
2
Recognize the retraction
Notice that the debater abandons his first view and adopts its opposite without stubbornness. Ibn Aqil says this happens when he sees what follows from his initial position.
3
Compare with stubbornness
Contrast this with al-mukabara: here the debater does not persist; he yields. The passage states it is 'below stubbornness' because he sees the implication and retracts.
Stubbornness involves denying a rational necessity even when it is clear.
4
Assess the interruption
Determine that it is still an interruption (inqita') because the debater undertook to support a claim but failed to complete the argument with it. He is considered 'munqati'' with respect to his first statement.
The interruption is due to inability to sustain the defense, not mere change of opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الانقطاع بمناقضة المذهب :
ونعني بها أن المجادل قد يعجز عن مناصرة قول له يذهب إليه في المسألة، فينصرف إلى القول بغيره دون أن يكابر عن الرأي الأول. قال ابن عقيل : وهو عجز عن الاستتمام بالحجة إلى المناقضة، وهو دون المكابرة، إذ قد يرى ما يلزمه على القول الأول فيرجع إلى نقيضه، ولا يكابر فيه . وإنما كان انقطاعا لأنه لما ضمن النصرة لشيء فلم يمكنه حتى عدل إلى خلافه، كان ذلك عجزا عن استتمام الحجة به. ويعتبر منقطعا في القول الأول، لأنه تخلى عنه، فيكون كمن عجز عن الدفاع عن رأي ما فأعلن تسليمه برأي مخالفه.
B - Interruption by contradiction of school:
We mean by it that the debater may become unable to support a statement he initially holds in the issue, so he turns to another statement without being stubborn about the first opinion. Ibn Aqil said: It is inability to complete the argument to the point of contradiction, and it is below stubbornness, since he may see what follows from the first statement and then retract to its opposite, without being stubborn in it. It is only considered interruption because when he undertook to support something and could not, until he turned to its opposite, that was inability to complete the argument with it. And he is considered interrupted with respect to the first statement, because he abandoned it, so he is like one who was unable to defend a certain opinion and declared his acceptance of his opponent's opinion.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines a milder form of interruption: contradicting one's own school (munaqadat al-madhhab). Unlike stubbornness, the debater does not persist but admits failure and abandons his original position. Ibn Aqil is quoted to contrast it with stubbornness: it is less severe because the debater recognizes the logical consequence and retracts, rather than denying it. However, it is still an interruption because he could not uphold his initial claim to the end.

Key Terms
مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Aqil, contradiction of school (al-munaqada) is below stubbornness because the debater:
A) Denies a rational necessity.
B) Persists in falsehood.
C) Retracts after seeing the implication.
D) Shifts to shouting.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction of school (al-munaqada) is a form of interruption where the debater abandons his stated view after seeing its logical consequences, falling short of stubbornness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a hypothetical jadal session on the Mu'tazili position on qadar, the Mutakallim initially argues that human actions are created by man, but after the opponent presents a Qur'anic verse, he retracts and says actions are created by Allah. Classify this as al-munaqada al-madhhab or not, and explain why.
?

Identifying Noise-Making (Mushāghabah) in Jadal
Author defines noise-making using Jurjani and Juwayni, showing its deceptive nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the deceptive nature of noise-making (mushāghabah) in debate and distinguish it from valid argumentation using the definitions of al-Jurjani and al-Juwayni.

Core Idea
Noise-making uses premises resembling accepted opinions to disrupt rational discourse.

In disciplined debate (jadal), there are rules of engagement. One disruptive tactic is noise-making (mushāghabah), where a debater abandons real reasoning and turns to shouting and repetition. This lesson uses the definitions of two great scholars—al-Jurjani and al-Juwayni—to help you spot and understand this fallacy, so you can uphold the integrity of scholarly argument.

Guided Path
1
Extract al-Jurjani's definition
Identify the core of noise-making: premises that are 'similar to accepted opinions' (mashhūrāt). These are not genuinely accepted but deceptively resemble them.
Accepted opinions are those commonly held, not necessarily true.
2
Analyze al-Juwayni's description
Notice the shift: the debater moves from legitimate inference (istidlāl) or disconnection (infiṣāl) to noise (shaghab), repetition (taraddad), shouting (ṣiyāḥ), and overpowering (ghalaba). This is an 'obscene interruption' (inqiṭāʿ fāḥish).
The Arabic verb 'intaqala' implies a deliberate abandonment of proper method.
3
Connect the two definitions
Al-Jurjani gives the logical structure (deceptive premises), while al-Juwayni shows the behavioral manifestation (shouting and repetition). Together they define the fallacy as both intellectual and procedural.
Noise-making is not just loudness; it often uses false arguments that sound plausible.
4
Distinguish from valid jadal
Compare mushāghabah with legitimate debate rules. Valid jadal requires premises from accepted premises (mashhūrāt) but used truthfully, not deceptively. Noise-making perverts this by using similar-looking but false premises.
The Mālikī scholar Ibn Rushd, in his commentary on Aristotle's Topics, also warns against such tactics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - الانقطاع بالمشاغبة :
والمشاغبة ؛ كما عرفها الجرجاني : هي مقدمات متشابهات بالمشهورات. قال الجويني : ومن الانقطاع الفاحش ؛ أن ينتقل مما هو فيه من الاستدلال أو الانفصال إلى الشغب والتردد بالصياح والغلبة.
C - Interruption by noise-making:
Noise-making, as defined by al-Jurjani, is 'premises similar to accepted opinions.' Al-Juwayni said: Among the obscene interruptions is that one shifts from what he is in of inference or disconnection to noise and repetition by shouting and overpowering.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines the third type: interruption by noise-making (mushāghabah). Al-Jurjani defines it as premises that resemble accepted opinions (mashhūrāt) but are not truly valid. Al-Juwayni describes a practical manifestation: the debater leaves legitimate inference and moves to shouting and repetition. This is considered an obscene (fāḥish) form of interruption because it abandons rational discourse for mere noise.

Key Terms

مقدمات متشابهات بالمشهورات
الانقطاع الفاحش

A) Denying a school.

C) Remaining silent in confusion.
D) Contradicting one's own opinion.

Key Takeaway
Noise-making (mushāghabah) is an illegitimate interruption that uses deceptive premises resembling accepted opinions and often turns to shouting and repetition to disrupt rational debate.

Four Types of Inqita' (Defeat) in Jadal
Author enumerates four types of defeat in dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the four types of defeat (inqita') in jadal based on the disputant's behavior.

Core Idea
Defeat in dialectic is not just silence—it includes obstinacy, contradiction, sophistry, and irrelevant inquiry.

In the classical Islamic dialectical tradition, a disputant is considered defeated (munqati') not only when he cannot respond, but also when he resorts to certain improper behaviors. This lesson examines the four types of defeat as enumerated by the author, providing a framework for recognizing when an argument has effectively ended.

Guided Path
1
Identify Obstinacy (Mukabara)
Look for a disputant who persists in a false claim despite clear evidence, denies his own previous statements, or denies necessary truths. This is the worst and most blameworthy form of defeat.
Examples include denying one's own words or rejecting self-evident truths.
2
Identify Self-contradiction (Munaqada)
Observe if a disputant abandons his initial position and adopts its opposite without being obstinate. This shows inability to defend the original claim, a lesser but clear defeat.
Ibn 'Aqil compares this to someone who cannot complete the proof and turns to the opposite view.
3
Identify Sophistry (Mushaghaba)
Notice if a disputant uses ambiguous premises resembling well-known truths, or resorts to shouting and verbal trickery when unable to provide sound proofs. This is considered egregious defeat.
Al-Jurjani defines it as premises resembling well-known things; al-Juwayni mentions moving to squabbling and shouting.
4
Identify Unnecessary Inquiry (Istifsar)
Observe if a disputant, when overwhelmed by proofs, asks irrelevant or unjustified questions merely to buy time. Legitimate inquiry is only for ambiguous statements; otherwise, it signals defeat.
If the opponent's speech is clear, such questioning is not a valid defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذلك يعتبر منقطعًا كل مجادل يعجز عن الغلبة باستعمال بالحجة ويتخذ المكابرة سبيلا له، وهو شر وجوه الانقطاع، وأقبحها وأدلها على سخف صاحبه، وقلة مبالاته بما يظهر من فضيحته وتخليطه في ديانته، وليس يُنتفع بكلام كانت عادته أن يحمل نفسه على المكابرة. والمكابرة تعرض للخصم في أمور : منها : أن يقول شيئًا ، فإذا رأى ما يلزم عليه جحد أن يكون قاله وصمم على ذلك وكابر فيه، وهذا الضرب من المكابرة يقع كثيرًا بين الخصوم. ومنها : أن يجحد مذهبًا له أو الرئيس الدي ينتحل قوله . ومنها : أن يجحد ضرورة يشترك أهل العقول فيها، ويدعي أن الحقيقة معه جحدها، وإنما يطلقون ما أطلقوا من إثباتها على جهة المجاز دون الحقيقة». ب - الانقطاع بمناقضة المذهب : ونعني بها أن المجادل قد يعجز عن مناصرة قول له يذهب إليه أولا المسألة، فينصرف إلى القول بغيره دون أن يكابر عن الرأي الأول. قال ابن عقيل : وهو عجز عن الاستتمام بالحجة إلى المناقضة، وهو دون المكابرة، إذ قد يرى ما يلزمه على القول الأول فيرجع إلى نقيضه، ولا يكابر فيه . وإنما كان انقطاعا لأنه لما ضمن النصرة لشيء فلم يمكنه حتى عدل إلى خلافه، كان ذلك عجزا عن استتمام الحجة به» منقطعا و بعتبر في القول الأول، لأنه تخلى عنه، فيكون كمن عجز عن الدفاع عن رأي ما فأعلن تسليمه برأي مخالفه . ج - الانقطاع بالمشاغبة : والمشاغبة ؛ كما عرفها الجرجاني (هي مقدمات متشابهات بالمشهورات» قال الجويني : ومن الانقطاع الفاحش ؛ أن ينتقل مما هو فيه من الاستدلال أو الانفصال إلى الشغب والتردد بالصياح والغلبة» ومعنى ذلك أن المجادل لا يمكنه أن يدافع عن رأيه بالأدلة والحجج المنطقية السليمة الواضحة، فيحاول أن يستعمل الشبهات والمقدمات غير المتكاملة منطقيا لكي يحافظ على وضع المجادل القوي. فإذا ما فعل هذا اعتبر عاجزا عن نصرة مذهبه، وبالتالي صار منقطعًا ومغلوبًا على أمره، ولم يكن مستحقا لمواصلة الجدل معه في المسألة . فإن كان المشغب مسؤولا قيل له : إن أجبت عن المسألة زدنا عليك، وإن لم تجب عنها أمسكنا عنك .. وإن كان سائلا قيل له : إن حصلت سؤالا سمعت جوابًا، وإلا فإن الشغب لا يستحق جوابًا، فإن لج وتمادى في غيه أعرض عنه، لأن أهل العلم إنما يتكلمون على ما فيه حجة أو شبهة، فإذا عري الجدل عن الأمرين إلى الشعب لم تكن فيه فائدة، وكان الأولى بذي الرأي الأصيل والعقل الرصين أن يصون نفسه عنه، ويرغب بقوته عن التضييع معه، ولاسيما إذا كان في الاشتغال به ما يوهم الحاضرين أن صاحبه سالك لطريق الحجة، فإنه ربما كان في ذلك شبه بما يرى منه من حسن العبارة واغترار بإقبال خصمه عليه في المناظرة فحق مثل هذا أن يُبَيَّنَ أنه على جهة المشاغبة دون طريق الحجة أو الشبهة . الانقطاع بالاستفسار : والاستفسار، معناه أن يلجأ المجادل - إذا ما ضاق عليه الحال ولم يعد قادرًا على الاستمرار في الجدل - إلى أن يلقي على مخالفه الذي حاصره بالحجج والبراهين جملة من الاستفهامات والاستفسارات غير المبررة، بغرض إعطاء فرصة لنفسه لكي يجمع عناصر الموضوع في ذهنه ويحاول استحضار أدلة وحجج جديدة ينصر بها مذهبه . وإنما يصح الاستفسار لكلام المخالف إذا كان فيه فعلا ما هو مبهم ويستحق أن يستفهم عنه، أما إن كان الكلام واضحًا وكانت الحجج التي يتضمنها منطقية يفهمها كل مستمع، فإن المخالف إذا لجأ حينئذ إلى الاستفسار اعتُبر منقطعًا.
Therefore, every disputant who is unable to prevail by using the proof and takes obstinacy as his way is considered defeated. It is the worst kind of defeat, the ugliest, and the clearest indication of the foolishness of its practitioner and his lack of concern for the disgrace and confusion that appear in his religion. No benefit is gained from speech from one whose habit is to burden himself with obstinacy. Obstinacy occurs to the opponent in matters: among them: that he says something, then when he sees what follows from it he denies having said it, insists on that, and persists in it obstinately. This type of obstinacy occurs frequently among opponents. Among them: that he denies a doctrine of his own or of the leader whose statement he adopts. Among them: that he denies a necessary truth shared by people of intellect, claiming that the truth is with him in denying it, and that what they affirm of it is only metaphorical, not real. B - Defeat by contradicting one's doctrine: we mean by it that the disputant may be unable to support a statement he initially held in the issue, so he turns to another statement without being obstinate about the first opinion. Ibn 'Aqil said: It is an inability to complete with proof, leading to contradiction, and it is lesser than obstinacy, since he may see what follows from the first statement and returns to its opposite, and he is not obstinate about it. It is considered defeat because he undertook to support something and could not, so he turned to its opposite; that is an inability to complete the proof with it. So he is considered defeated in the first statement because he abandoned it, like one who is unable to defend an opinion so he declares his acceptance of his opponent's opinion. C - Defeat by sophistry: Sophistry, as defined by al-Jurjani, is 'premises resembling well-known things.' Al-Juwayni said: Among egregious defeat is that one moves from what he is in of argumentation or rebuttal to squabbling and repetition with shouting and overpowering. The meaning is that the disputant cannot defend his opinion with sound, clear logical proofs and arguments, so he tries to use doubts and logically incomplete premises to maintain the position of a strong disputant. If he does this, he is considered unable to support his doctrine, thus he becomes defeated and overcome in his affair, and is not worthy of continuing debate on the issue. If the squabbler is a respondent, he is told: If you answer the question, we will add to you; if you do not answer it, we will refrain from you. If he is a questioner, he is told: If you produce a question, you will hear an answer; otherwise, squabbling does not deserve an answer. If he persists and continues in his error, he is ignored, because the people of knowledge only speak about what contains a proof or a doubt. If debate is stripped of both matters to squabbling, there is no benefit in it, and it is better for one of sound opinion and solid intellect to protect himself from it and to refrain from wasting time with it, especially if engaging in it gives the attendees the impression that its practitioner is following the path of proof, for perhaps there is in that some resemblance to what is seen of good expression and being deceived by the opponent's acceptance of him in discussion. It is right that such a person be shown to be in a state of sophistry, not on the path of proof or doubt. Defeat by inquiry: Inquiry means that the disputant, when the situation becomes tight for him and he is no longer able to continue in debate, resorts to casting upon his opponent who has overwhelmed him with proofs and evidences a set of unjustified questions and inquiries, with the purpose of giving himself an opportunity to gather the elements of the topic in his mind and try to bring to mind new proofs and arguments with which to support his doctrine. Inquiry into the opponent's speech is only valid if there is actually something ambiguous in it that deserves to be asked about. But if the speech is clear and the proofs it contains are logical and understood by every listener, then if the opponent resorts to inquiry at that point, he is considered defeated.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of defeat (inqita') in dialectical argumentation (jadal). The author enumerates four types: obstinacy (mukabara), self-contradiction (munaqadat al-madhhab), sophistry (mushaghaba), and unnecessary inquiry (istifsar). The passage explains each in order. First, obstinacy: a debater who cannot prevail with sound arguments resorts to obstinacy, which is the worst form of defeat. It can take various forms, such as denying one's own previous statement or denying a necessary truth. Second, self-contradiction: when a debater abandons his original position and adopts the opposite, showing inability to defend his initial claim. This is considered defeat because he fails to complete the proof for his doctrine. Third, sophistry: the debater uses ambiguous premises and verbal trickery instead of clear arguments. The text quotes al-Jurjani and al-Juwayni to define sophistry and notes that once identified, the sophist should be warned or ignored. Fourth, inquiry: when a debater, overwhelmed by his opponent's proofs, resorts to asking irrelevant questions to buy time. The passage clarifies that legitimate inquiry is only for ambiguous statements; if the speech is clear, such questioning is a sign of defeat. This classification helps identify when a debater has effectively lost the argument, even if he has not explicitly conceded.

Key Terms
انقطاع
مكابرة
مناقضة المذهب
مشاغبة
استفسار

Identifying Shaghab and Its Consequences in Jadal
Author explains that sophistry (shaghab) leads to disconnection (inqita') and outlines how to deal with the sophist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a debater is engaging in shaghab (sophistry) and determine the appropriate response based on the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
Shaghab is the death of dialectical debate: once it appears, the sophist forfeits their right to continue.

In disciplined Islamic debate, the integrity of argumentation is paramount. This passage from a jadal manual defines shaghab—the use of fallacious arguments when unable to defend one's position—and its direct consequence: inqita', or being considered disconnected and unworthy of further debate. Understanding this rule is essential for anyone engaging in or evaluating scholarly disputation in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the behavior of shaghab
Look for a debater who, unable to defend his opinion with clear logical proofs, resorts to ambiguous premises or sophistries (shubhat). For example, in a fiqh debate, if a respondent cannot provide a solid textual basis and instead uses a weak analogy, that is shaghab.
Shaghab is distinct from a legitimate shubhah (semblance of argument)—it is a deliberate tactic to mask inability.
2
Understand the consequence: inqita'
Once a debater engages in shaghab, he is considered 'munqati'' (disconnected) and incapable of supporting his school. The debate may be terminated with respect to him.
3
Apply the rules based on the sophist's role
If the sophist is the respondent (mujib), he is given a chance: answer correctly and the debate continues, or remain silent and the debate stops. If he is the questioner (sa'il), his question is dismissed; only legitimate questions deserve answers.
This prevents the debate from being hijacked by irrelevant or fallacious challenges.
4
Know when to disengage completely
If the sophist persists and continues in error, the proper response is to turn away from him. The passage explains that scholarly debate only has value when there is a valid argument or a plausible shubhah; if neither exists, continuing is futile and may mislead observers.
This protects the integrity of the debate and prevents the sophist from gaining credibility through mere persistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى ذلك أن المجادل لا يمكنه أن يدافع عن رأيه بالأدلة والحجج المنطقية السليمة الواضحة، فيحاول أن يستعمل الشبهات والمقدمات غير المتكاملة منطقيا لكي يحافظ على وضع المجادل القوي. فإذا ما فعل هذا اعتبر عاجزا عن نصرة مذهبه، وبالتالي صار منقطعًا ومغلوبًا على أمره، ولم يكن مستحقا لمواصلة الجدل معه في المسألة . فإن كان المشغب مسؤولا قيل له : إن أجبت عن المسألة زدنا عليك، وإن لم تجب عنها أمسكنا عنك .. وإن كان سائلا قيل له : إن حصلت سؤالا سمعت جوابًا، وإلا فإن الشغب لا يستحق جوابًا، فإن لج وتمادى في غيه أعرض عنه، لأن أهل العلم إنما يتكلمون على ما فيه حجة أو شبهة، فإذا عري الجدل عن الأمرين إلى الشعب لم تكن فيه فائدة، وكان الأولى بذي الرأي الأصيل والعقل الرصين أن يصون نفسه عنه، ويرغب بقوته عن التضييع معه، ولاسيما إذا كان في الاشتغال به ما يوهم الحاضرين أن صاحبه سالك لطريق الحجة، فإنه ربما كان في ذلك شبه بما يرى منه من حسن العبارة واغترار بإقبال خصمه عليه في المناظرة فحق مثل هذا أن يُبَيَّنَ أنه على جهة المشاغبة دون طريق الحجة أو الشبهة (1).
The meaning of that is that the debater cannot defend his opinion with sound, clear logical proofs and arguments, so he tries to use sophistries and logically incomplete premises in order to maintain the position of a strong debater. If he does this, he is considered incapable of supporting his school, and thus becomes disconnected and overcome in his affair, and is not deserving of continuing the debate with him on the issue. If the one creating confusion is a respondent, it is said to him: 'If you answer the question, we will increase upon you; if you do not answer it, we will stop with you.' If he is a questioner, it is said to him: 'If you pose a question, you will hear an answer; otherwise, sophistry does not deserve an answer.' If he persists and continues in his error, turn away from him, because the people of knowledge only speak about what has an argument or a sophistical semblance. So if the debate is stripped of both and turns to sophistry, it has no benefit, and it is more appropriate for the person of sound opinion and solid intellect to protect himself from it and to spare his strength from wasting with it, especially if engaging in it might give the impression to those present that its practitioner is following the path of argument. For perhaps there is in that a semblance due to what is seen of his good expression and the delusion of the opponent's turn toward him in the debate. So it is right for such a one to be made clear that he is in the manner of sophistry, not in the way of argument or semblance (1).
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines shaghab (sophistry) as the use of fallacious arguments when unable to defend one's view with sound logic. It declares that resorting to shaghab renders the debater 'munqati'' (disconnected) and unworthy of continuing. The author then provides practical rules: if the sophist is the respondent, he is given a chance to answer properly or be dismissed; if he is the questioner, his question is ignored. Should he persist, the debaters should turn away from him. The rationale is that scholarly debate only proceeds when either a valid argument or a plausible sophistical semblance is present; when neither remains, the discussion becomes futile. The passage concludes by warning against engaging in such empty debate, as it can mislead observers into thinking the sophist is following the path of argument. The focus is on preserving the integrity of dialectical engagement.

الانقطاع
المشغب

Key Takeaway
Shaghab (sophistry) disqualifies a debater from continuing the debate; the proper response is to either give him a chance to answer properly or dismiss him, and if he persists, turn away.

Inqita' bi al-Istifsar: Disconnection by Requesting Clarification
Author defines disconnection by requesting clarification and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish legitimate istifsar from disingenuous stalling in a jadal exchange, and identify when a debater's request for clarification indicates disconnection (inqita').

Core Idea
Requesting clarification is only legitimate when the opponent's speech is genuinely ambiguous; otherwise, it exposes the debater's inability to continue.

In this passage, the author defines the second type of disconnection in dialectic: inqita' bil-istifsar. He explains that istifsar, when used improperly, becomes a cover for weakness. The passage also quotes Ibn 'Aqil to elaborate on the legitimate uses of clarification. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to spot when a debater is using istifsar as a stalling tactic versus a genuine request for clarification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim about istifsar
What does the author say motivates a debater to request clarification when overwhelmed? Locate the description of resorting to 'unjustified interrogatives' to gain time.
The author states the debater is no longer able to continue, so he feigns confusion.
2
Extract the conditions for legitimate istifsar
According to the author, when is it permissible to ask for clarification? Find the sentence starting with 'إنما يسوغ'.
Legitimate istifsar is limited to cases of ambiguity, shared meaning, or obscure expression.
3
Analyze Ibn 'Aqil's commentary
What three situations does Ibn 'Aqil allow for istifsar? How does he describe the disingenuous form?
He mentions ambiguity, shared meaning, and obscure expressions; otherwise it is 'بطالة وإطالة' (frivolity and prolongation).
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical scenario
Given a debate snippet, determine whether a request for clarification is legitimate or indicates disconnection based on the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الانقطاع بالاستفسار : والاستفسار، معناه أن يلجأ المجادل - إذا ما ضاق عليه الحال ولم يعد قادرًا على الاستمرار في الجدل - إلى أن يلقي على مخالفه الذي حاصره بالحجج ،والبراهين جملة من الاستفهامات والاستفسارات غير المبررة، بغرض إعطاء فرصة لنفسه لكي يجمع عناصر الموضوع في ذهنه ويحاول استحضار أدلة وحجج جديدة ينصر بها مذهبه . وإنما يصح الاستفسار لكلام المخالف إذا كان فيه فعلا ما هو مبهم ويستحق أن يستفهم عنه، أما إن كان الكلام ،واضحًا وكانت الحجج التي يتضمنها منطقية يفهمها كل مستمع، فإن المخالف إذا لجأ حينئذ إلى الاستفسار اعتُبر منقطعًا . قال ابن عقيل عن هذا النوع من الانقطاع : وهو عجز عن الاستتمام بطلب الاستفسار في غير موضعه، وذلك إذا ضاق على الخصم الكلام مال إلى استفهام ما لا يستفهم عن مثله، واستفسار ما لا يستفسر في حال المناظرة . فقال : ما معنى كذا؟ وما معنى كذا؟ وهو استرواح إلى جري عبارته بدلا من سكتة يخجل بها ويتضح بها انقطاعه، فيبدل السكوت بالاستفسار إيهاما للحاضرين أنه إذا فسر لي معنى هذا تكلمت عليه، وإنما المعيق لي عن الكلام عدم فهم معناه أو يحيد عن النظر، فيتعلق بذلك التعلق المتحير وإنما يسوغ الاستفسار والمراجعة فيما يتردد المعنى فيه ويشترك، فأما عدم التردد والاشتراك فلا وجه للاستفسار، أو يكون في العبارة نوع تغيير وإغماض، فيطلب تفسيرها بالأكشف، وما خرج عن هذه الأقسام فالاستفسار عنه بطالة وإطالة (1).
Disconnection by Requesting Clarification: Clarification means that the debater, when the situation becomes tight for him and he is no longer able to continue in the debate, resorts to throwing upon his opponent, who has besieged him with arguments and proofs, a set of unjustified interrogatives and requests for clarification, with the purpose of giving himself an opportunity to gather the elements of the subject in his mind and try to recall new proofs and arguments to support his school. Clarification of the opponent's speech is only valid if there is actually something in it that is ambiguous and deserving of inquiry. But if the speech is clear and the arguments it contains are logical and understood by every listener, then if the opponent resorts to clarification at that point, he is considered disconnected. Ibn 'Aqil said about this type of disconnection: It is an inability to complete the debate by requesting clarification in its improper place. That is, when speech becomes tight for the opponent, he inclines to ask about things that are not inquired about, and requests clarification about things that are not asked about in the state of debate. So he says: 'What does such-and-such mean? What does such-and-such mean?' And it is a way to breathe through the flow of his speech instead of a silence by which he would be ashamed and by which his disconnection would become clear. So he substitutes silence with clarification to give the impression to those present that 'if he explains to me the meaning of this, I will speak about it,' and that the impediment to my speech is not understanding its meaning. Or he deviates from consideration and clings to that confused clinging. Requesting clarification and review is only allowed where the meaning is ambiguous and shared; as for cases where there is no ambiguity or shared meaning, there is no basis for clarification. Or if the expression has some alteration or obscurity, then one may request its explanation in clearer terms. Whatever falls outside these categories, clarification about it is frivolity and prolongation (1).
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of disconnection: inqita' bil-istifsar. It explains that istifsar is a tactic used by a debater who is overwhelmed and unable to continue, feigning confusion to gain time. The author then states the legitimate condition for requesting clarification: the opponent's speech must genuinely be ambiguous. If the speech is clear and logical, requesting clarification shows disconnection. Ibn 'Aqil elaborates that this is an inability to complete the debate by asking about what should not be asked, substituting silence with questions to mislead observers. He clarifies that legitimate istifsar is limited to ambiguous or shared meanings, or obscure expressions; otherwise, it is frivolous. The passage distinguishes between permissible inquiry and disingenuous stalling.

Key Terms
الاستفسار
الانقطاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is requesting clarification (istifsar) legitimate in debate according to this passage?
A) When the opponent is strong.
B) When the speech is ambiguous or the meaning is shared.
C) When the debater needs time to think.
D) When the audience is confused.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, requesting clarification is only a legitimate move when the opponent's speech is genuinely ambiguous; otherwise, it exposes the debater's disconnection.

Disconnection by Resorting to Acceptance
Author defines disconnection by acceptance and shift to branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth type of disconnection (inqita' bir-ruju' ila at-taslim) in a jadal exchange and explain why shifting the debate to a branch indicates inability to defend the original claim.

Core Idea
Accepting the opponent's claim to shift the debate is a disguised surrender, not a victory.

In dialectical debate (jadal), a debater may appear to concede a point by accepting the opponent's claim (taslim) while trying to redirect the discussion to a subsidiary issue. This lesson examines when such a move actually reveals a failure to defend the original position. The passage defines this as a form of disconnection (inqita'), and Ibn 'Aqil clarifies the underlying weakness.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two types of taslim
Identify whether the acceptance is dialectical (for argument's sake) or acknowledgment (concession). The passage mentions both: 'تسليم جدل أو تسليم اعتراف'.
Dialectical acceptance is a common tactic; here it is used to avoid the main dispute.
2
Locate the original issue and the branch
The passage states that the real dispute is about the original (الأصل), not the branch (الفرع). When the debater shifts discussion to the branch, that shift is the key signal.
The branch is a subsidiary point the debater tries to use to refute the opponent.
3
Apply Ibn 'Aqil's criterion
Ibn 'Aqil says the debater is unable to complete what he accepted (عجز عن الاستتمام بما سلم). Instead of contesting the connection between original and branch, he avoids it.
The proper response would be to challenge the link, not to accept and then evade.
4
Determine disconnection
If the debater fails to defend the original and instead pursues a branch after taslim, he is disconnected (منقطع). The passage explicitly links the shift to inability: 'فهو حين نقل الكلام من الأصل إلى الفرع دل ذلك على عجزه عن نصرة أصله'.
This type of disconnection is a tactical error, not necessarily a logical fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - الانقطاع بالرجوع إلى التسليم: والتسليم معناه أن يسلم المجادل لمخالفه دعواه أو دليله تسليم جدل أو تسليم ،اعتراف وغرضه أن يثبت له أن هذه الدعوى لا تثبت أو أن دليله لا ينتج ما بناه عليه فيفرع عليه ما ينقضه به، مع أن الخلاف في الأصل وليس في الفرع، فهو حين نقل الكلام من الأصل إلى الفرع دل ذلك على عجزه عن نصرة أصله، فكان بذلك منقطعًا. قال ابن عقيل في توضيح هذا النوع من الانقطاع : هو عجز عن الاستتمام بما سلم إلى الرجوع عنه، وسواء كان ذلك تسليم جدل أو تسليم اعتراف لأن الخصم إنما يسلم تسليم الجدل ليوقع المنازعة في التفريع عن الأصل دون الأصل، وذلك أنه لما كان له أن ينازع في شهادة الأصل بالفرع إذا آثر الكلام في أحدهما دون الآخر وتضمن أن يكسر المقالة من جهته دون کسرها من الوجه الآخر، فكان عليه استتمام ذلك، وإلا فقد ظهر عجزه عما تضمنه، وانقطاعه عما يظن أنه بلغه (2).
H - Disconnection by Resorting to Acceptance: Acceptance means that the debater concedes to his opponent his claim or his proof, either as a dialectical acceptance or an acknowledgment acceptance, aiming to show him that this claim is not established or that his proof does not produce what he based on it, so he branches off onto it what would refute it, while the dispute is in the original and not in the branch. So when he transfers the discussion from the original to the branch, that indicates his inability to support his original, and thus he becomes disconnected. Ibn 'Aqil said in explanation of this type of disconnection: It is an inability to complete what he had accepted by recanting from it. And whether it is a dialectical acceptance or an acknowledgment acceptance, because the opponent only makes a dialectical acceptance to instigate contention in the branching off from the original, not in the original itself. That is because he could have contested the testimony of the original to the branch if he had preferred speech in one over the other, and it entailed breaking the argument from his side without breaking it from the other side. So he should have completed that; otherwise, his inability regarding what it entailed becomes apparent, and his disconnection from what he thought he had reached (2).
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of disconnection: inqita' bir-ruju' ila at-taslim. Taslim here means the debater accepts the opponent's claim or proof (either for the sake of argument or as outright acknowledgment) in order to shift the debate to a subsidiary issue, aiming to refute it there. However, since the real dispute lies in the original issue, this shift reveals the debater's inability to defend his original position, thus he is considered disconnected. Ibn 'Aqil explains that this is an inability to complete what he had accepted; by accepting and then trying to question the branch, he avoids the original. The proper course would have been to contest the connection between original and branch, but his failure to do so shows his weakness. The passage underscores the importance of defending the core claim directly.

Key Terms
التسليم (al-taslīm): Accepting the opponent's claim or proof, either dialectically (for argument's sake) or as acknowledgment, then shifting to a subsidiary point. [Note: In jadal, taslim can be a legitimate dialectical move, but here it is used as a tactic to avoid the main issue, indicating disconnection.]
الانقطاع (al-inqiṭāʿ): Disconnection caused by inability to defend the original position, shown by shifting debate to a branch. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does resorting to taslim (acceptance) in debate indicate according to this passage?
A) The debater is confident in his own argument.
B) The debater is unable to defend his original position.
C) The debater is employing a valid dialectical strategy.
D) The debater is following the rules of proper debate.

Key Takeaway
Shifting the debate from the original issue to a branch after accepting the opponent's claim reveals the debater's inability to defend the original position, constituting a specific type of disconnection (inqita') in jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate transcript, identify an instance where a debater accepts the opponent's claim and then shifts the discussion to a different issue. Determine whether this constitutes inqita' bir-ruju' ila at-taslim, and explain why using the criteria from the passage.
?
Role-play a jadal exchange: One student takes the position that 'prayer is obligatory for every Muslim' (original claim). The opponent argues that 'prayer is only obligatory for those who are sane'. The first student accepts this (taslim) and then shifts to discussing the definition of sanity. Analyze whether this move indicates disconnection according to Ibn 'Aqil's explanation.

Identifying Inappropriate Clarification Requests in Debate
Defines the first type of dialectical failure: seeking clarification where it is not warranted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a clarification request in a dialectical exchange is legitimate or a sign of disconnection.

Core Idea
Not every question in debate is genuine — asking for clarification on clear points is a tactic to hide weakness.

In this passage, the author describes a specific dialectical failure: seeking clarification where it is not warranted. This occurs when a debater, constrained in argument, resorts to asking about meanings that are already clear, thereby masking his inability to proceed. The author provides criteria to distinguish legitimate clarification requests from those that are idle and obstructive. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone engaged in formal debate (munazara), especially in Islamic sciences where precise argumentation is valued.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debater's situation
Recognize when a debater is cornered or constrained in the argument. The passage says this happens when 'the opponent is constrained in speech' (ضاق على الخصم الكلام).
2
Examine the clarification request
Note whether the request is for something already clear or commonly understood. The author states it is inappropriate when the meaning is 'not ambiguous or shared' (لا تردد ولا اشتراك).
3
Apply the author's exceptions
Check if the request falls under legitimate cases: genuine ambiguity, shared meaning, or obscure expression. If none of these, the request is a sign of disconnection.
The passage lists: 'something whose meaning is ambiguous or shared' or 'alteration or obscurity in expression'.
4
Assess the function of the request
Determine whether the request serves to advance understanding or to cover embarrassment. The author notes it is used to avoid silence (سكته) that would reveal breakdown.
This step distinguishes sincere inquiry from tactical evasion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو عجز عن الاستتمام بطلب الاستفسار في غير موضعه، وذلك إذا ضاق على الخصم الكلام مال إلى استفهام ما لا يستفهم عن مثله، واستفسار ما لا يستفسر في حال المناظرة . فقال : ما معنى كذا؟ وما معنى كذا؟ وهو استرواح إلى جري عبارته بدلا من سكتة يخجل بها ويتضح بها انقطاعه، فيبدل السكوت بالاستفسار إيهاما للحاضرين أنه إذا فسر لي معنى هذا تكلمت عليه، وإنما المعيق لي عن الكلام عدم فهم معناه أو يحيد عن النظر، فيتعلق بذلك التعلق المتحير وإنما يسوغ الاستفسار والمراجعة فيما يتردد المعنى فيه ويشترك، فأما عدم التردد والاشتراك فلا وجه للاستفسار، أو يكون في العبارة نوع تغيير وإغماض، فيطلب تفسيرها بالأكشف، وما خرج عن هذه الأقسام فالاستفسار عنه بطالة وإطالة
And it is inability to complete [the argument] by seeking clarification in an inappropriate place. That is, when the opponent is constrained in speech, he inclines to asking about what is not usually asked, and seeking clarification of what is not sought clarification about in the situation of debate. So he says: 'What does this mean? And what does that mean?' And it is a seeking of ease for his expression instead of a silence that would embarrass him and make his breakdown evident. So he substitutes silence with seeking clarification, leading the audience to think: 'If he explains this meaning to me, I will speak about it; the only thing preventing me from speaking is my lack of understanding of its meaning,' or he veers from inquiry, clinging to such a confused attachment. Clarification and questioning are only permissible regarding something whose meaning is ambiguous or shared. As for what is not ambiguous or shared, there is no basis for seeking clarification. Or if there is some alteration or obscurity in the expression, he asks for an clearer interpretation. Whatever falls outside these categories, seeking clarification about it is idle and tiresome.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of dialectical failure (inqita') known as 'seeking clarification inappropriately' (al-inqita' bi talab al-istifsar). The author explains that this occurs when a debater is cornered and, instead of conceding or remaining silent, starts asking irrelevant questions about terms that are already clear. This tactic is a cover for his inability to continue the argument. The author distinguishes between legitimate and illegitimate requests for clarification: legitimate when the meaning is ambiguous or the expression is obscure; illegitimate when the meaning is clear and well-understood. This classification guards against the misuse of clarification as a delaying or diversionary tactic.

Key Terms
الاستفسار (al-istifsār): Seeking clarification or asking for explanation, but here specifically the inappropriate use of such requests to cover weakness. [Note: Stable term across jadal texts; always denotes request for clarification.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Legitimate clarification if the definition is complex
B) Inappropriate clarification signifying breakdown
C) A proper method to gain time
D) A sign of deep critical thinking

Key Takeaway
Inappropriate clarification requests in dialectic are a cover for inability to proceed; they are identifiable by the absence of genuine ambiguity or obscurity.

Disconnection by Mutual Abuse
Author defines the fourth type of disconnection: mutual abuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dialectical exchange has degenerated into mutual abuse and explain why it indicates argumentative failure.

Core Idea
When insults replace arguments, the debate has already ended.

In the classical tradition of jadal, the goal is to establish truth through reasoned discourse. But what happens when tempers flare and insults fly? The scholars recognized this as a formal breakdown, not a mere loss of etiquette. This lesson defines al-musabba and its significance as a sign of disconnection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline condition
The passage states that debate remains acceptable as long as mutual respect is maintained. Find the phrase that describes this condition.
Look for 'في إطار الاحترام المتبادل'.
2
Detect the transition to abuse
The passage says that if the scientific aspect recedes and is replaced by verbal violence, that indicates disconnection. Identify the phrase that marks this shift.
The phrase 'تراجع الجانب العلمي وحل محله العنف اللفظي' signals the breakdown.
3
Understand the implication
Why does this indicate disconnection? Because resorting to abuse is a sign of weakness and an attempt to conceal lack of argument. The passage explicitly states this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ز - الانقطاع بالمسابة : إن الجدل يبقى على صورته العلمية المقبولة ما دام يجري في إطار الاحترام المتبادل بين الطرفين المتجادلين بحيث يحفظ كل منهما قدر صاحبه ولا يتعدي عليه بأي صورة من صور العنف اللفظي أما إن تراجع الجانب العلمي وحل محله العنف اللفظي دل ذلك على انقطاع المجادل الدي لجأ إلى هذه الوسيلة لإخفاء ضعفه. قال الجويني : وأفحش من الشغب أن يصيرا أو أحدهما، إلى المسابة . وقال ابن عقيل:
Z - Disconnection by mutual abuse: Debate remains in its acceptable scientific form as long as it proceeds within the framework of mutual respect between the two debating parties, such that each preserves the dignity of the other and does not transgress against him in any form of verbal violence. But if the scientific aspect recedes and is replaced by verbal violence, that indicates the disconnection of the debater who resorted to this means to conceal his weakness. Al-Juwayni said: 'And more obscene than disturbance is that they both, or one of them, descend to mutual abuse.' And Ibn 'Aqil said:
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of dialectical failure: al-inqita' bi al-musabba (disconnection by mutual abuse). The author states that debate remains valid as long as mutual respect is maintained. If the scientific content is replaced by verbal violence, it shows that the debater is disconnected and is using abuse to hide his weakness. The passage cites two authorities: al-Juwayni declares that abuse is even worse than mere disturbance, and Ibn 'Aqil is cited (though his statement is cut off). This type emphasizes the ethical dimension of jadal: maintaining respect is essential for the argument to be considered valid.

Key Terms
المسابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A strong emotional investment in the issue
B) The debater's disconnection and attempt to hide weakness
C) An acceptable rhetorical strategy
D) A shift to a higher level of argument

Key Takeaway
Mutual abuse in debate is a formal indicator of failure and disconnection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Preserving Adab in Jadal: Why Verbal Abuse Invalidates Argument
Author defines the fourth type of disconnection: mutual abuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a debate has maintained its 'scientific form' (al-sura al-'ilmiyya) based on the presence of respect or abuse.

Core Idea
A debate is only valid when conducted with mutual respect.

The passage sets a clear ethical boundary: respect is not optional but constitutive of valid debate. This lesson explores how adab (etiquette) is not just a moral extra but a condition for the argument to be taken seriously. Without it, the exchange loses its scientific character and becomes mere verbal conflict.

Guided Path
1
Extract the condition for valid debate
Find in the passage the exact phrase that sets the condition for debate to remain in its scientific form.
It is 'ما دام يجري في إطار الاحترام المتبادل'.
2
Contrast scientific discourse with abuse
The passage contrasts two states: scientific discourse and verbal violence. Identify the two contrasting terms used.
العلمي vs. العنف اللفظي.
3
Apply the condition to a hypothetical
If a debater starts insulting, does the debate retain its scientific form? According to the passage, no. The debate is considered disconnected.

Disconnection by Denying One's Own School in Jadal
Defines disconnection by denying one's own school in jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the error of jahd al-madhhab in a jadal exchange and explain why it leads to disconnection.

Core Idea
Refuting your opponent does not prove your own position; failing to defend your school first disconnects you.

In jadal (dialectical debate), there is a correct procedure: you must first establish the soundness of your own school with clear proofs before attacking your opponent's position. If you skip this and try to win only by demolishing the other side, you commit a fallacy called jahd al-madhhab (denying your own school). This lesson unpacks that error using a vivid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correct order in jadal
According to the passage, what is the proper sequence? First, the disputant must establish his own school with clear proofs that compel the opponent to agree. Only then may he critique the opponent's school.
2
Recognize the fallacy of jahd al-madhhab
If the disputant refuses to speak about his own school and instead tries to prove his position solely by demolishing the opponent's arguments, he is disconnected. The logic is flawed: destroying the opponent's case does not automatically validate one's own.
The opponent might be correct but unprepared to defend.
3
Analyze the analogy of the house builder
The author compares the incorrect disputant to a man who cannot build his own house except by first demolishing a neighbor's house. This reveals his inability to build independently—just as the disputant cannot prove his own school on its own merits.
The analogy illustrates dependence on negativity.
4
Apply the rule to a real debate scenario
Consider a debate between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i on qiyas. If the Hanafi only attacks the Shafi'i's proofs without defending his own usul, he commits jahd al-madhhab. His conclusion that his school is correct does not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و - الانقطاع بجحد المذهب : فالمجادل ؛ الأصل فيه أن يثبت صحة مذهبه بإقامة الأدلة الواضحة عليه، والتي تلزم الخصم بالتسليم والموافقة ثم إذا ما انتقد بعد ذلك مذهب مخالفه وأثبت ضعف أدلته كان عمله سليما. أما إن رفض أن يتحدث عن مذهبه أو أدلته هو، وأراد أن يثبت صحة مذهبه بهدم مذهب مخالفه أولا، اعتبر بذلك منقطعًا، لأن هدم مذهب مخالفه لا يعني بالضرورة - أن مذهبه هو صحيح أو أدلته قوية لأنه ربما كان مذهب مخالفه الصحيح، ولكن هذا المخالف لم يكن لديه الاستعداد العلمي الكافي للدفاع عن رأيه. والمثل في ذلك، والمثل له من طريق الصورة رجل ضمن على نفسه بناء بيت تسلم مع بنائه أبنية ،مجاوريه، فلم يمكنه أن يبنيه إلا بهدم بيت يليه لبعض مجاوريه، فهو لا محالة منقطع عن بلوغ ما قدر . فكذلك الخصم إنما يكلم خصمه على سلامة مذاهبه عنده، وإذا جحد شيئًا إما على جهة الرجوع وإما على جهة المكابرة فيه، كان منقطعًا في حكم الجدل (1) .
And - disconnection by denying the school: The disputant; the principle therein is that he establishes the correctness of his school by presenting clear proofs for it, which obligate the opponent to submission and agreement. Then if, after that, he criticizes the school of his opponent and proves the weakness of his proofs, his action is sound. But if he refuses to speak about his own school or his proofs, and wants to establish the correctness of his school by demolishing the school of his opponent first, he is thereby considered disconnected, because demolishing the school of his opponent does not necessarily mean that his own school is correct or his proofs are strong, for perhaps the opponent's school was correct, but this opponent did not have sufficient scientific preparation to defend his opinion. The example of that, and the example by way of analogy, is a man who undertook to build a house while receiving the buildings of his neighbors, and he could not build it except by demolishing the house of some of his neighbors; he is inevitably disconnected from achieving what he intended. Thus the opponent only addresses his opponent concerning the soundness of his schools with him, and if he denies something either by way of retreat or by way of obstinacy, he is disconnected in the judgment of jadal (1).
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage defines a specific form of disconnection (inqita') in dialectical debate (jadal): disconnection by denying one's own school (jahd al-madhhab). The correct method is first to establish the soundness of one's own position with clear proofs, compelling the opponent to acknowledge it. Only then should one critique the opponent's arguments. If a disputant instead tries to prove his position solely by refuting the opponent, without first defending his own, he is considered 'disconnected' because destroying the opponent's case does not automatically validate one's own; the opponent might be right but unprepared. The author gives a concrete analogy: a man who wants to build a house but can only do so by first demolishing a neighbor's house; this shows his inability to achieve his own goal independently. Likewise, a disputant who denies his own school (either by retreating or stubbornly refusing) is disconnected in the judgment of jadal.

الجدل

Disconnection by Insulting in Jadal
Defines disconnection by insulting in jadal with authoritative quotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between legitimate critique of a school and personal attack in jadal, and identify when a disputant has become disconnected by insulting.

Core Idea
Insulting signals disconnection: the debater has abandoned proof for personal attack.

In Islamic dialectic, the goal is to establish truth through respectful exchange. This passage explains that when a disputant resorts to verbal abuse, it indicates they are disconnected from their own argument and are trying to hide weakness. Al-Juwayni and Ibn 'Aqil clarify that personal attacks are categorically different from legitimate critique of a school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline of acceptable jadal
Note the condition: jadal remains acceptable as long as mutual respect is maintained. This sets the standard for proper debate.
The phrase 'في إطار الاحترام المتبادل' is key.
2
Recognize the sign of disconnection
When verbal violence replaces scientific discussion, it indicates that a disputant is disconnected and using insults to hide weakness. This is the signal (inșila').
3
Distinguish between legitimate critique and personal attack
Ibn 'Aqil differentiates: attacking the opponent's school with proofs is necessary and praiseworthy; attacking the opponent or his predecessors on unrelated matters is objectionable and constitutes disconnection.
The objectionable thing (al-munkar) is the personal attack, not the doctrinal critique.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Imagine a debate: a Hanafi criticizes a Shafi'i position by citing weak evidence. That is critique. But if he then insults the Shafi'i scholar's lineage, he has disconnected by insulting. Identify the boundary.
Use real madhhab differences, e.g., rulings on fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ز - الانقطاع بالمسابة : إن الجدل يبقى على صورته العلمية المقبولة ما دام يجري في إطار الاحترام المتبادل بين الطرفين المتجادلين بحيث يحفظ كل منهما قدر صاحبه ولا يتعدي عليه بأي صورة من صور العنف اللفظي أما إن تراجع الجانب العلمي وحل محله العنف اللفظي دل ذلك على انقطاع المجادل الدي لجأ إلى هذه الوسيلة لإخفاء ضعفه. قال الجويني : وأفحش من الشغب أن يصيرا أو أحدهما، إلى المسابة (2) . وقال ابن عقيل : إذا انتهى الجدل إلى المسابة دل على أن الذي حمله على ذلك، ضيق عطنه وانقطاعه عن حجته، وليس السبب أن يظهر فيه إنكار المذهب الذي قصد إلى الطعن عليه وإقامة الحجة على إفساده لأنه لابد من ذلك والدلالة على صحة ما يقوله فيه، وإنما المنكر الطعن على الخصم أو على أسلافه بما ليس من المذاهب والاختلاف فيها في شيء.. وإذا فعل أحد الخصمين شيئا من ذلك، له أن ما أتى به خارج عن حد السؤال والجواب إلى السباب، ولم يكن الاجتماع للمسابة وإنما كان لإقامة الحجة وحل الشبهة» (1) .
Z - Disconnection by insulting: Indeed, jadal remains in its acceptable scientific form as long as it proceeds within the framework of mutual respect between the two disputing parties, such that each preserves the status of the other and does not transgress against him in any form of verbal violence. But if the scientific aspect recedes and is replaced by verbal violence, that indicates the disconnection of the disputant who resorted to this means to conceal his weakness. Al-Juwayni said: 'And more obscene than chaos is that they, or one of them, resort to insulting (2).' Ibn 'Aqil said: 'When jadal ends in insulting, it indicates that what drove him to that is narrowness of his mind and his disconnection from his proof. It is not because there appears in it denial of the school that he intended to attack and to establish proof of its corruption, for that is necessary, and to indicate the correctness of what he says about it. Rather, the objectionable thing is the attack on the opponent or on his predecessors concerning matters that are not related to schools and differences therein... If one of the two opponents does something of that sort, he should know that what he has done is outside the bounds of question and answer and into insulting. The gathering was not for insulting, but rather for establishing proof and resolving doubts.' (1)
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage defines disconnection by insulting (al-inqita' bi-al-musabba) in jadal. It begins by stating that jadal remains acceptable as long as mutual respect is maintained. If verbal violence replaces scientific discussion, it signals that the disputant is disconnected and using insults to hide weakness. The author then quotes al-Juwayni, who emphasizes that resorting to insulting is even more obscene than general chaos. Ibn 'Aqil elaborates: when debate ends in insults, it indicates the disputant's mental narrowness and inability to continue with proof. He distinguishes between legitimate critique of the opponent's school (which is necessary in debate) and illegitimate personal attacks on the opponent or his predecessors regarding matters unrelated to the doctrinal differences. Such behavior moves the discussion from proper question-and-answer into mere abuse, contradicting the purpose of the gathering, which was to establish proofs and resolve doubts.

الانقطاع
الشغب

Insulting in debate is not just rude—it signals the debater's disconnection from his own argument and undermines the entire purpose of jadal, which is to establish truth through respectful proof and counter-proof.

Transition in Jadal: Praiseworthy vs Blameworthy
Defines transition and begins classification into praiseworthy and blameworthy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a transition (intiqal) in a jadal exchange and classify it as praiseworthy or blameworthy based on the criteria from this passage.

Core Idea
Not every change of topic is a disconnect; some transitions strengthen the argument.

In the previous lesson we examined disconnection (inqita') as a dialectical fault. Now we turn to transition (intiqal), a specific type of apparent disconnection that may be either blameworthy or praiseworthy. The author defines transition, quotes Ibn 'Aqil's definition, and begins listing the conditions under which a transition is not considered a fault. Understanding this distinction will enable you to evaluate the legitimacy of a debater's move to a new line of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of transition
Read the passage carefully: transition is leaving what one is doing (whether reasoning or answering) before completing it, to move to something else. Ibn 'Aqil calls it an 'inability to complete the first'.
Note the two subtypes: from reasoning (i'tilal) or from one issue to another.
2
Distinguish the two types: praiseworthy and blameworthy
The author divides transition into two categories: praise-worthy (mahmud) which is not real disconnection, and blameworthy (madhmum) which is actual disconnection. Identify the defining criterion: does the transition serve the original point or abandon it?
The first example: when asked to correct a relevant point, building on sound premises is not blameworthy because the debater remains committed to his initial position.
3
Identify the characteristics of praiseworthy transition
The text provides one complete aspect: a) When the debater is required to correct something he relied upon, so he builds on that correction to strengthen his original argument. The transition is only in form, not in reality, because he is still supporting what he started.
The passage cuts off, but expect more aspects in the original source.
4
Apply the classification to a new example
Consider a hypothetical jadal exchange: A debater argues that riba in barter applies to wheat. The opponent asks for clarification of the 'illa (effective cause). The debater then moves to define the 'illa of riba. Is this a blameworthy transition?
No, because he is correcting the foundation of his argument, staying on topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الانتقال هو أحد أبرز أنواع الانقطاع ومعناه أن يترك أحد المتجادلين ما هو فيه من استدلال أو جواب على اعتراض إلى غيره قبل أن يتمه. وقد عرفه ابن عقيل فقال : هو عجز عن الإتمام للأول للخروج إلى الثاني، وذلك في الانتقال عن الاعتلال قبل الاستتمام، أو الانتقال عن مسألة إلى مسألة أخرى قبل تمام الأولى ) (2) . وقال في موضع آخر: «الانتقال عن السؤال هو الخروج عما يوجبه أوله من ملازمة السَّنَنِ فيه، وكذلك الانتقال عن الجواب» (3) . وهو على ضربين محمود ليس بانقطاع، ومذموم هو انقطاع. أولا : الانتقال المحمود الذي ليس بانقطاع : وهو على وجوه : أ - أن يطالب بتصحيح شيء تعلق به فبناه على ما يصح بناؤه عليه وانتقل إلى تصحيحه ليبني ما وقعت فيه المطالبة عليه. فهذا في الصورة انتقال، ولا يعد في الحقيقة، لأنه لم يترك ما ابتدأ بنصرته، بل هو في صميم ذلك غير
The second requirement: Transition (al-intiqal) is one of the most prominent types of disconnection. Its meaning is that one of the two debaters leaves what he is in of reasoning or answering an objection to something else before completing it. Ibn 'Aqil defined it by saying: 'It is an inability to complete the first to move to the second, and that is in transitioning from reasoning before completion, or transitioning from one issue to another issue before finishing the first (2).' He said in another place: 'Transition from the question is departing from what its beginning requires of adherence to its rules, and likewise transition from the answer (3).' It is of two types: praiseworthy, which is not disconnection, and blameworthy, which is disconnection. First: The praiseworthy transition which is not disconnection. It has several aspects: a) That one is asked to correct something to which he is attached, so he builds upon what is valid to build upon it, and he transitions to its correction in order to build what the demand occurred about. So this is in form a transition, but it is not considered in reality, because he did not leave what he began to support, rather he is in the core of that, without...
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major topic: transition (al-intiqal). It is defined as leaving an incomplete line of reasoning or answer to move to another before finishing. Ibn 'Aqil defines it as an inability to complete the first, which manifests either in abandoning a proof before its completion or moving from one issue to another before finishing the first. He also characterizes transition from the question as departing from the required adherence to the rules. The author then classifies transition into two types: praiseworthy (mahmud) and blameworthy (madhmum). Praiseworthy transition is not considered true disconnection. The first example given: when a debater is asked to correct something relevant, so he builds on sound premises and transitions to correct it to complete the original point. In form it appears as transition, but in reality he is still supporting what he started, so it is not disconnection. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with other aspects.

Key Takeaway
Transition in jadal is not inherently a fault; it is blameworthy only if it truly abandons the original line, while praiseworthy transition serves the original argument.
